class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D3122%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DTianshuai%2BWang%252C%2BJiale%2BQu%252C%2BDominik%2BLegut%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D19%26issueNum%3D5%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.9b00544%26title%3DUnique%2BDouble-Interstitialcy%2BMechanism%2Band%2BInterfacial%2BStorage%2BMechanism%2Bin%2Bthe%2BGraphene%252FMetal%2BOxide%2Bas%2Bthe%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D3130%26publicationDate%3DMay%2B2019" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/asset/images/medium/nl-2019-00544t_0005.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/asset/images/large/nl-2019-00544t_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Graphene/metal oxides (G/MO) composite materials have attracted much attention as the anode of sodium ion batteries (SIBs), because of the high theoretical capacity. However, most metal oxides operate based on the conversion mechanism and the alloying mechanism has changed to Na<sub>2</sub>O after the first cycle. The influence of G/Na<sub>2</sub>O (G/N) on the subsequent sodiation process has never been clearly elucidated. In this work, we report a systematic investigation on the G/N interface from both aspects of theoretical simulation and experiment characterization. By applied first-principles simulations, we find that the sluggish kinetics in the G/MO materials is mainly caused by the high diffusion barrier (0.51 eV) inside the Na<sub>2</sub>O bulk, while the G/N interface shows a much faster transport kinetics (0.25 eV) via unique double-interstitialcy mechanism. G/N interface possesses an interfacial storage of Na atom through the charge separation mechanism. The experimental evidence confirms that high interfacial ratio structure of G/N greatly improves the rate performance and endows G/MO materials the interfacial storage. Furthermore, the experimental investigation finds that the high interfacial ratio structure of G/N also benefits from the reversible reaction between SnO<sub>2</sub> and Sn during cycling. Lastly, the effects of (N, O, S) doping in graphene systems at the G/N interface were also explored. This work provides a fundamental comprehension on the G/MO interface structure during the sodiation process, which is helpful to design energy storage materials with high rate performance and large capacity.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D3122%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DTianshuai%2BWang%252C%2BJiale%2BQu%252C%2BDominik%2BLegut%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D19%26issueNum%3D5%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.9b00544%26title%3DUnique%2BDouble-Interstitialcy%2BMechanism%2Band%2BInterfacial%2BStorage%2BMechanism%2Bin%2Bthe%2BGraphene%252FMetal%2BOxide%2Bas%2Bthe%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D3130%26publicationDate%3DMay%2B2019" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright Â© 2019 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290830&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diffusion" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Diffusion" class="article__tags__link">Diffusion</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290817&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Interfaces" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Interfaces" class="article__tags__link">Interfaces</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292506&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Materials" class="article__tags__link">Materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292522&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Two dimensional materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Two%20dimensional%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Two dimensional materials</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20battery">sodium ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Metal+Oxide-graphene&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_metal%20oxide-graphene">metal oxide-graphene</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Double-interstitialcy+Diffusion&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_double-interstitialcy%20diffusion">double-interstitialcy diffusion</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Interface+Storage&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_interface%20storage">interface storage</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodiation+Kinetics&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodiation%20kinetics">sodiation kinetics</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">With the development of economy and society, the ever-increasing demand for high-energy-density storage systems for portable electronics and electric vehicles has stimulated intensive research on rechargeable batteries. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1â3)</a> Among various available energy storage technologies, lithium-ion battery (LIBs) has been used as the dominant power source of electronic equipment for decades, because of its high safety, environmental friendliness, long cycle life, and so on. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 4 to 6">(4â6)</a> However, the further large-scale commercial manufacturing of LIBs may face rigorous challenges from the increasing cost of lithium, because of its rare reserves on Earth. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> Sodium ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as one of the most promising alternatives to LIBs for application in energy storage devices, because of sodium being an abundant natural resource (2.64 wtâ¯% on Earth), environmentally friendly, and SIBs having similar physical and chemical properties as LIBs, including ionicity, electronegativity, and electrochemical activity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 8 and 9">(8,9)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">However, because of the fact that the ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> is much larger than that of Li<sup>+</sup>, many intercalated negative electrode materials, which can lead to the excellent electrical performance for LIBs, are not suitable to host Na<sup>+</sup> ions, because of the limited interstitial space. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 10 to 13">(10â13)</a> Therefore, an exploration on suitable anode material is critical to realize SIBs with excellent performance. Conversion-type negative materials have been applied successfully in LIBs and exhibit excellent performance, but rarely explored for SIBs until the conversion reaction between spinel NiCo<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and Na were observed by AlcÃ¡ntara et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> Later, many studies reported the conversion-type materials containing the metal oxide can also serve as promising anode in SIBs, such as Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, MoO<sub>3</sub>, Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, and SnO<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 15 to 17">(15â17)</a> This reaction, involving multiple electron exchange, is characterized by a relatively high theoretical specific capacity. With the development of the carbon materials and the advent of graphene, the poor electrical conductivity and large volume change during the chargeâdischarge process of metal oxides can be improved by forming a composite structure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 18 and 19">(18,19)</a> However, the further practical application of the SIBs still faces the challenge of the conversion reaction sluggish kinetics, which results in a low capacity rate. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The storage mechanism in the graphene/metal oxides (G/MO) nanocomposite materials involves the decomposition of metal oxides and the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>O during the charge process, which can be described by the following equation: <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 21 and 22">(21,22)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/asset/images/medium/nl-2019-00544t_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i3"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>Theoretically, such reaction can induce the formation of the graphene/Na<sub>2</sub>O (G/N) interfaces in the sodiation stage of G/MO nanocomposites. As the sodiation process continues, the nonconductive Na<sub>2</sub>O layer will expand uniformly on the metal oxide surface, which is analogous to the role of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) generated by the reaction with liquid electrolyte. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> As a result, further sodiation process is predicted to be different from the initial stage, which is closely related to the kinetic performance of the sodium along the interface of G/N. In addition, interface engineering plays a vital role in the storage mechanism of battery field, while it has been repeatedly reported that the interface storage mechanism exists in the transition-metal oxide electrode for LIBs or SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 24 to 26">(24â26)</a> Zhukovskii et al. realized the high electrode capacity via a heterogeneous storage mechanism by Ru/Li<sub>2</sub>O. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> Wang et al. found that the Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>âSnS<sub>2</sub> heteronanostructure can accelerate the conversion reaction and provide the extra capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> Qin et al. found that the SnSb/N-doped graphene hetero interface can offer extra interfacial sodium storage and exhibit much enhanced rate performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Liu et al. revealed that the pseudocapacity-like behavior originates at the interface of graphene/TiO<sub>2</sub> from the atomic level theoretical calculations. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> Numerous studies have been aimed at the storage behavior and rate performance of the hybrid structure electrode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 31 and 32">(31,32)</a> However, the investigation of interface behavior from G/MO phase to the G/N after the sodiation process received minimal attention. Because of the inevitable existence of the G/N interface in the sodiation process of the metal oxide materials, it can obviously be assumed that the electrochemical performance of G/MO materials is influenced by G/N interface. Therefore, it is important to understand the kinetic behavior and storage mechanism at the G/N interface.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, we report a systematic investigation on the interface features of G/MO materials during the sodiation process by performing first-principles simulations. Based on these theoretical modeling, we found that the sluggish kinetics of metal oxides is primarily caused by the diffusion transferring through the Na<sub>2</sub>O bulk phase, while the diffusion barrier of Na ion on the G/N interface can be greatly reduced through the novel double-interstitialcy mechanism. Moreover, the pseudocapacity-like interface storage mechanism behavior of Na ion at G/N interface during the sodiation process is also elucidated by the electronic structure analysis. Such characteristics on high rate performance and interfacial storage give foresight for the advantage of designing a G/MO modeling with high interfacial ratio. On the basis of theoretical design, we further verified this theoretical design by fabricating the G/SnO<sub>2</sub>/G hollow structures as the sodium anode. Experimental results demonstrated that the materials exhibit excellent rate performance and interfacial storage, which is highly consistent with the calculated results. In addition, our experimental results show that the high interfacial structure also benefits the partially reversible reaction between SnO<sub>2</sub> and Sn during cycling. In addition, we explored the (N, O, S) doping effect at the G/N interface, and further optimize the interfacial structure. This study, which combines electronic structure simulations with experiment, provides novel insight of the mechanism for the diffusion and storage behaviors of Na ions at the G/MO materials. It clearly exhibits its advantage to design a G/MO modeling with high interfacial ratio, which will be useful to achieve the SIBs with high energy density and high rate performance.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">2.  Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.1.  Computation Method</h3><div class="NLM_p">The projector augmented wave formalism of density functional theory, as implemented in the Vienna ab initio Simulation Package, is used in the system energy and electronic structure calculations. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> Generalized gradient approximation in the form of PerdewâBurkeâErnzerhof was used as the exchange-correlation functional. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> In order to get a better description of weak physical interactions, we choose a computationally cost-effective optB86b-vdw functional, so that vdW interactions could be taken into account. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> The width of smearing was chosen as 0.05 eV. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> The energy cutoff for plane-wave expansion is 600 eV. The optimized lattice constants of graphene and Na<sub>2</sub>O are 2.47 and 5.54 Ã, which is in good agreement with previous computational results and experiment data. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37 ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 37 and 38">(37,38)</a> Graphene with a 5 Ã 5 supercell can fit the Na<sub>2</sub>O (111) surface (3 Ã 3 supercell), and the lattice mismatch is 4.7% when forming the (doped) graphene/Na<sub>2</sub>O interface structure, as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. One doping atom was substituted for one C atom in the doped graphene, corresponding to the doping concentration of 3.1%. In the vertical direction, a vacuum layer 15 Ã thick is introduced for all of the surfaces and interfaces. The Brillouin zone is sampled using the MonkhorstâPack scheme with a <i>k</i>-point mesh of 3 Ã 3 Ã 1 with the Gamma-centered grids for the structural relaxation, and 9 Ã 9 Ã 1 <i>k</i>-point mesh is used for the static calculations of all of the systems. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> The structure relaxation is continued until the forces on all of the atoms are converged to &lt;0.01 eV/Ã. Transition-state structures and associated minimum energy pathways for interfacial Li diffusion were computed with the climbing image-nudged elastic band (CI-NEB) method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 40 and 41">(40,41)</a></div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/asset/images/medium/nl-2019-00544t_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/asset/images/large/nl-2019-00544t_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Top view of the XG/N interface model, where X represents N, O, and S atoms; numbers â1â, â2â, and â3â indicate the sites above which Na atoms are adsorbed at the Na<sub>2</sub>O surface and G/Na<sub>2</sub>O interface, which are Na or O atoms in the first, second, and third atomic layer, respectively. (b) Charge density difference of G/N with 1 Na atom. (c) Charge changes of the Na atom in the Na<sub>2</sub>O surface, G/N interface, and graphene in the G/N interface. (d) Relationship between the sodiation voltage and the amount of Na adatoms (capacity) at G/N interfaces. (e) Diffusion energy for Na atom diffusion through the Na<sub>2</sub>O bulk, and (f) G/N interface. The inset images represent the schematic diagram of the diffusion pathway. The green ball (labelled as âIâ) represents the interstitial Na atom, and the blue ball (labelled as âSâ) means the Na atom located in the lattice of Na<sub>2</sub>O.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/asset/images/large/nl-2019-00544t_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.2.  Experiments</h3><div class="NLM_p last">To synthesize the C@SnO<sub>2</sub>@C composite, 15 g of NaCl, 2.5 g of citric acid (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>8</sub>O<sub>7</sub>), and 0.79 g of SnCl<sub>4</sub> were dissolved in 100 mL of distilled water. After stirring for 6 h, the homogeneous solution was freeze-dried under the condition of vacuum and â43 Â°C to obtain the precursor. Subsequently, the precursor spread on a small corundum tube was annealed at 650 Â°C for 1 h under a C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>2</sub>/Ar atmosphere (5% C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>2</sub>) and then cooled to room temperature. After washing with distilled water, C@SnO<sub>2</sub>@C composite was obtained. XRD measurements were performed on a Rigaku D/max diffractometer with a Cu KÎ± radiation at a wavelength of 1.5406 Ã. SEM and TEM were performed on JEOL Model JSM-6700F, FEI Tecnai Model G2 F20, and JEOL Model JEM-2100Plus systems. Electrochemical measurements were conducted using a coin-type test cell (CR2032). For fabrication of cells, the working electrode was composed of C@SnO<sub>2</sub>@C, sodium carboxymethyl cellulose, and carbon black in a weight ratio of 8:1:1 on a copper foil. The electrolyte was 1.0 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC and DEC (volume ratio, 1:1) CV measurements were conducted at 0.2â1.8 mV/s within 0.01â3.0 V on a Princeton electrochemical workstation. Galvanostatic charge/discharge cycles were tested by a LAND CT2001A electrochemical workstation at various current densities, from 0.1 A/g to 10 A/g.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">3.1.  Pseudocapacity-Like Storage and Priority Diffusion Mechanism in the Graphene/Na<sub>2</sub>O</h3><div class="NLM_p">First, the interfacial interaction between the graphene and Na<sub>2</sub>O was investigated. The interface interaction energy is defined as <i>E</i><sub>I</sub> = <i>E</i><sub>G</sub> + <i>E</i><sub>NO</sub> â <i>E</i><sub>G/N</sub>, where <i>E</i><sub>G</sub>, <i>E</i><sub>NO</sub>, and <i>E</i><sub>G/N</sub> represent the energies of the (doped) graphene, Na<sub>2</sub>O, and (doped) graphene/Na<sub>2</sub>O interfaces, respectively. The electron density of graphene was redistributed after the formation of G/N interface (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/suppl_file/nl9b00544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a> in the Supporting Information), while the Bader charge analysis shows quantitatively that there is some electron migration (0.64 e) from the outermost Na layer to the graphene layer. Electrons mainly accumulate in the interfacial areas due to the electropositive character of the outermost Na<sup>+</sup> layer of Na<sub>2</sub>O surface. Through the electron localization function (ELF) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> analysis (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/suppl_file/nl9b00544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a> in the Supporting Information), it can be clearly seen that no covalent bond exists between the graphene and Na<sub>2</sub>O, because the ELF value at the interface is â¼0. Combining with the per unit area interfacial interaction energy results (18 meV Ã<sup>â2</sup>), the interaction between the graphene and Na<sub>2</sub>O can be confirmed to be of vdW type.</div><div class="NLM_p">Subsequently, the interfacial storage behavior of Na adatom at the G/N interface is investigated. In order to evaluate the storage ability of the G/N interface quantitatively, we compare the adsorption energy of Na adatom with the cohesive energy of the Na atom in sodium metal, and define the difference between them as the sodiation potential value (<i>E</i><sub>v</sub>). While a positive <i>E</i><sub>v</sub> means that the sodium adatom contributes to the excess storage capacity. The adsorption energy of Na atoms is defined as <i>E</i><sub>d</sub> = â(<i>E</i><sub>T</sub> â <i>E</i><sub>0</sub> â <i>nE</i><sub>Na</sub>)/<i>n</i>, where <i>E</i><sub>T</sub>, <i>E</i><sub>0</sub>, and <i>E</i><sub>Na</sub> are the energies of interfacial system intercalated by <i>n</i> Na atoms, the energy of the pristine interfacial system, and the energy of the single free Na atom, respectively. The average voltage (<i>E</i><sub>v</sub>) is defined as <i>E</i><sub>v</sub> = â(<i>E</i><sub>T</sub> â <i>E</i><sub>0</sub> â <i>nE</i><sub>Naâ¯bulk</sub>)/<i>ne</i>, where <i>e</i> is the absolute value of the electron charge and <i>E</i><sub>Naâ¯bulk</sub> is the energy of one Na atom in the Na body-centered cubic (bcc) bulk. Calculated results show that the most stable adsorption site for one Na atom at the G/N interface is Site 3, with a sodiation potential of +0.62 V, which is much bigger than that of one sodium adsorption at the Na<sub>2</sub>O surface (â0.38 V, Site 2). It seems that the graphene endows the Na<sub>2</sub>O enhanced adsorption ability of Na atom. To further investigate this interfacial storage mechanism, the electronic structure analysis of the most stable adsorption configuration was performed. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b, the introduction of interfacial Na atom causes more charge transfer toward the graphene layer. Bader charge analysis (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c) revealed that the graphene layer of the G/N interface gains 1.13 e after the interfacial Na atom is inserted, which is bigger than the value (0.64 e) forming the G/N interface. Moreover, interfacial Na adatom losses 0.82 e, which is much greater than the value of 0.26 e on the Na<sub>2</sub>O surface. As a result, much stronger interaction between the interfacial adatom and the G/N interface can be induced. The electrons transfer from the Na adatom to the graphene layer and Na<sub>2</sub>O layer when Na atoms are adsorbed at the G/N interface. The partial density of states (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/suppl_file/nl9b00544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a> in the Supporting Information) for the <i>s</i>-orbital of the interfacial Na atom and <i>p</i>-orbital of the nearest neighboring O atom at Na<sub>2</sub>O at the G/N interface, and it can be clearly seen that these two states hybridize at the energy range from â2.0 eV to â0.6 eV. Such adsorption behavior clearly indicates that the Na<sub>2</sub>O can be served as the host for Na<sup>+</sup> cation, because of the binding between the O atom of Na<sub>2</sub>O and the interfacial Na<sup>+</sup> cation, while the graphene layer serves as an electron acceptor. Hence, spatial electronâcation separation can be depicted as a pseudocapacity-like interfacial mechanism. In addition, more additional Na atoms can be intercalated further at the interface region between graphene and Na<sub>2</sub>O surface. We gradually increase the number of interface Na atoms until the <i>E</i><sub>v</sub> value turns negative. Results show that the G/N interface system can adsorb 3 Na atoms corresponding to 0.099 mAh/m<sup>2</sup> (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d).</div><div class="NLM_p">During the sodiation process of G/MO anode materials, the diffusion of Na atoms involves two pathways. The first one is at the interface of G/N, while the second one is inside the Na<sub>2</sub>O bulk phase. Because of the high symmetry of the Na<sub>2</sub>O bulk phase and the big radius of the Na atom, the interstitial Na atom prefer to locate at the center of the octahedron zone composed of six nearby O atoms (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e). An interstitial atom can diffuse to the interstitial site without displacing lattice atoms via a direct-hopping mechanism. Alternatively, it can also diffuse into a host lattice site by knocking a neighboring lattice atom into an adjacent interstitial site (interstitialcy mechanism). Our calculated results show that the diffuse mechanism in the Na<sub>2</sub>O bulk phase occurs mainly via the latter mechanism, because of its much lower energetic barrier. The interstitial Na atom (marked by âIâ in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e) first move to the position of adjacent Na atom (marked by âSâ in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e) of normal lattice of Na<sub>2</sub>O bulk, while SâNa atom will simultaneously move to the center of the nearby octahedron. However, because of the high coordination number of the interstitial Na atom with adjacent O ions, this process shows a relatively high energetic diffusion barrier of 0.51 eV. We further measured the energy evolution for the diffusion of the Na atom at the Na<sub>2</sub>O surface, and the result shows that the diffusion barrier is 0.37 eV (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/suppl_file/nl9b00544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a> in the Supporting Information).</div><div class="NLM_p">At the G/N interfaces, the calculation results show that the Na ion at the interface will encounter an energy barrier as low as 0.25 eV (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f), which is smaller than the value in the Na<sub>2</sub>O surface or the Na<sub>2</sub>O bulk phase. Particularly, the Na ion prefers to follow a novel âVâ diffusion pathway to move to another stable site, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f. The migration behavior of single Na atom at the interface involves the simultaneous movement of Na atom adjacent to the first layer of the Na<sub>2</sub>O surface. However, such a cooperative mechanism is different from the interstitialcy mechanism. such that, in the bulk Na<sub>2</sub>O, and the diffusion can be realized by a two-step interstitialcy mechanism, which involves the cooperative motion by one interstitial Na atom and one host Na atom of the Na<sub>2</sub>O (111) surface. First, the interface Na atom (marked by âIâ in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f) will substitute the Na atom (marked by âSâ in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f) in the original lattice of the outermost layer of Na<sub>2</sub>O at the interface of G/N, while the present host Na atom âSâ will migrate to the adjacent interstitial site, which is different from the initial âIâ site. Second, the âIâ atom will continuously diffuse to the adjacent stable site, meanwhile the âSâ atom, now located in the new interstitial site, will diffuse back to its previous host site. The entire diffusion behavior involves two cooperation interstitialcy mechanism processes, in which the SâNa atom act as a temporary assistant role when the IâNa atom diffuse. From the perspective of the entire migration process, the interstitial atom and host atom does not change their own roles.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In order to reveal the mechanism of such unique interstitialcy mechanism, we further investigated the Bader charge of IâNa atom and SâNa atom at every diffusion image state (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/suppl_file/nl9b00544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a> in the Supporting Information). The Bader charge value results show that both of them exhibits a coordination change trend along the diffusion. Because of the ionization of the interface Na atom, its properties are similar to the first Na layer atoms of Na<sub>2</sub>O surface while both of them are bound with three adjacent O atoms, which is smaller than the value in bulk phase (six adjacent O atoms). In this situation, the migration of Na atoms at the interface of G/N results in low local lattice deformation. Downward migration of ions at the high-energy sites can offset a portion of the energy barrier of the upward migration of ions at low-energy sites. In addition, the concerted diffusion can also decrease the repulsion effect between the âIâ atom and âSâ atom. We also investigate Na ion diffusion when more Na ions are inserted into the interface; the results show that the magnitude of barrier is also very low, and the diffusion behavior is still similar to the two-step interstitialcy process (see detail discussion in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/suppl_file/nl9b00544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Section S1 and Figure S6</a> in the Supporting Information). On the basis of the barrier simulation, the diffusion constant <i>D</i> can be calculated, while the calculated diffusion constants at room temperature (300 K) for the systems are listed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/suppl_file/nl9b00544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a> in the Supporting Information. Diffusion along the G/N interface is the prioritized diffusive path when Na atom transfer to the active electrode materials occurs in the sodiation process. Combining both thermodynamic and kinetic factors, it is obvious to design materials that possess a high interface ratio of G/N, will highly enhance the rate performance, and improve the storage capacity. In the next section, we will verify this hypothesis and continue to investigate the influence of high G/N interface ratio on G/MO materials, together with experimental studies.</div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9">3.2.  Experimental Validation of High Interface Ratio Structure</h3><div class="NLM_p">In order to verify the smooth diffusion of Na along the G/N interface, unique structure of sandwiched C@SnO<sub>2</sub>@C composite was synthesized, in which SnO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals were confined in carbon shells, as shown in the schematic illustration of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a. Such a sandwiched structure design ensures that numerous interfaces between Na<sub>2</sub>O and the carbon sheet can be produced during sodiation. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c show SEM images of the C@SnO<sub>2</sub>@C composite, from which it can be seen that the cubic carbon shells are assembled together to form a micrometer-scale interconnected conductive network. The large pores on the surface of the carbon shell demonstrate its hollow structure, which contribute to the deep penetration of the electrolyte and shorten the diffusion path of ions through the solid phase. In addition, the smooth surface of carbon shells indicates that the SnO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals are all-around confined in the carbon shell, increasing the interface area between SnO<sub>2</sub> and carbon.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/asset/images/medium/nl-2019-00544t_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/asset/images/large/nl-2019-00544t_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) Schematic illustration of the sandwich-structured C@SnO<sub>2</sub>@C composite. (b, c) SEM images. (d, e) TEM images and EDS pattern (shown as the inset in panel (d) ) of the C@SnO<sub>2</sub>@C composite. (f) SAED pattern of the C@SnO<sub>2</sub>@C composite.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/asset/images/large/nl-2019-00544t_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The TEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d) confirms the hollow structure of the interconnected cubic C@SnO<sub>2</sub>@C, and the corresponding EDS pattern (inset in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d) represents the only existence of elemental C, O, and Sn. It can be seen from the HRTEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e) that the carbon shell has a wall thickness of â¼15 nm and is embedded with several 5 nm nanocrystals, which can be confirmed as SnO<sub>2</sub> from the XRD and SAED patterns (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f, as well as <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/suppl_file/nl9b00544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a> in the Supporting Information). Note that the SnO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals are completely embedded in the carbon shell and remain monodisperse, which, on the one hand, can prevent the high-energy surface of the active nanocrystals from directly contacting the electrolyte, and on the other hand, increases the two-phase interface between SnO<sub>2</sub> and carbon. In order to better understand the role of G/N interface in electrochemical reactions and its effect on the energy storage mechanism, coin cells with sodium metal as the counter electrode were employed to analyze the electrochemical behavior of C@SnO<sub>2</sub>@C electrode including cycle performance, rate performance, cyclic voltammetry and energy storage mechanisms.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b show the cycling and rate performance, from which it can be seen that the C@SnO<sub>2</sub>@C electrode delivers a high average capacity of â¼370 mAh/g at a current density of 0.1 A/g. Besides, as the current rates increase from 0.1 to 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 A/g, the capacities of the C@SnO<sub>2</sub>@C electrode are 338, 289, 237, 193, 146, and 105 mAh/g, respectively, exhibiting excellent rate performance and superior to most SnO<sub>2</sub>-based electrodes (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/suppl_file/nl9b00544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a> in the Supporting Information). The electrochemical reactions of the C@SnO<sub>2</sub>@C electrode can be inferred from the CV curve of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c. In the cathodic scan, a reduction peak of â¼0.68 V can be seen initially, which corresponds to the conversion of SnO<sub>2</sub> to Sn and it accompanies the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>O. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 43 to 45">(43â45)</a> Subsequently, two peaks, located at 0.23 and 0.05 V, are associated with the alloying reaction of Sn into Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sn and the intercalation reaction of carbon with Na. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> In the anodic scan, the peak at 0.29 V corresponds to the dealloying reaction of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sn. Notably, the broad peak at â¼1 V corresponds to the conversion reaction of Sn to SnO<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> As is generally known, the conversion reaction between Sn and SnO<sub>2</sub> is difficult to be reversible, so the sodium storage capacity of SnO<sub>2</sub> is unsatisfactory. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> However, it is considered that the SnO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals in C@SnO<sub>2</sub>@C electrode can be partially reversible during cycling and the entire electrochemical reaction can be illustrated as follows:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/asset/images/medium/nl-2019-00544t_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i10"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/asset/images/medium/nl-2019-00544t_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i11"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></span>Thus, to further verify this inference, structure and phase change of the electrode were investigated by TEM, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/suppl_file/nl9b00544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a> in the Supporting Information. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/suppl_file/nl9b00544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S9a and S9f</a> show the HRTEM images of the C@SnO<sub>2</sub>@C composite at sodiated state (0.25 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) and desodiated state (3.0 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), respectively. Through Fourier transform and lattice spacing analysis, SnO<sub>2</sub> is first converted to Sn and then sodiated to Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>, which is consistent with the reaction equation above (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/suppl_file/nl9b00544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9aâS9e</a> in the Supporting Information). Importantly, the conversion of SnO<sub>2</sub> to Sn results in the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>O, which is amorphous and dispersed around the Sn nanoparticles after several cycles. While, during desodiation process, the Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> is first dealloyed to form Sn, and from <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/suppl_file/nl9b00544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S9fâS9j</a> in the Supporting Information, it can be seen that some Sn nanoparticles can indeed be reversibly converted to SnO<sub>2</sub>. This confirms that SnO<sub>2</sub> is partially reversible in this structure, which may be attributed to the fact that SnO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals are completely confined in the carbon matrix, resulting in the formation of a large amount of Na<sub>2</sub>O/carbon and Na<sub>2</sub>O/SnO<sub>2</sub> interface during sodiation (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/suppl_file/nl9b00544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9aâS9e</a>). This can provide fast transfer channels for ions and electrons and promote the dynamic process of reversible conversion.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/asset/images/medium/nl-2019-00544t_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/asset/images/large/nl-2019-00544t_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Cycling performance. (b) Rate capability and (c) CV curves of the C/SnO<sub>2</sub>/C electrode. (d) CV curves at different scan rates. (e) Anodic scans of CV curves and the corresponding <i>b</i>-values.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/asset/images/large/nl-2019-00544t_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d shows the CV curves of C/SnO<sub>2</sub>/C electrode tested at various scan rates, from which it can be seen that, as the scan rate increases, the redox peaks gradually widen and the overall shape become more rectangular, indicating a capacitive energy storage behavior. In fact, the power law relationship between the scan rate <i>v</i> and the corresponding current <i>i</i> can be used as follows to measure the capacitive feature of electrodes: <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 48 and 49">(48,49)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq4"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/asset/images/medium/nl-2019-00544t_m004.gif" alt="" id="_i12"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></div></div></span>For <i>b</i> = 0.5, a diffusion-controlled energy storage process is suggested; while <i>b</i> = 1.0 represents a capacitive-controlled process, since a typical capacitor-like linear relationship between current and scan rate is obtained. A voltage range of 0.29â2 V in the anodic scan was selected to calculate the <i>b</i>-value, as it included two major oxidation peaks. It can be seen from the result shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e that the <i>b</i>-value approaches 0.5 in the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sn dealloying process (0.29 V), which shows the typical diffusion-controlled energy storage. However, as the dealloying reaction progresses, the <i>b</i>-value gradually increases and reaches a maximum value of 0.97 at the end of the reaction. Subsequently, as the conversion reaction of Sn to SnO<sub>2</sub> proceeds, the <i>b</i>-value decreases again until reaching its lowest value of 0.78. If <i>b</i> = 0.75 is used as the boundary between the two reaction mechanisms, then the conversion reaction of SnO<sub>2</sub> is always capacitive-controlled. The ultrafast electrochemical reaction brought by the capacitive-controlled process verifies why SnO<sub>2</sub> can be reversibly converted. In addition, the quantitative calculation of the contribution of capacitive controlled energy storage were also investigated (see the detailed discussion in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/suppl_file/nl9b00544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Section S2 and Figure S10</a> in the Supporting Information), the results show that this structure can indeed change its energy storage mechanism, thus accelerating the diffusion/conduction of ions/electrons and improving the rate performance of the electrode.</div><div class="NLM_p last">These experimental results confirm our hypothesis that the storage capacity mechanism and rate performance can be greatly regulated by designing electrode materials with high G/N interface ratio. In adition, we also find that the high G/N interface structure is improving the reversible conversion of the active material during electrode cycling.</div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13">3.3.  Doping Effect on Interface Storage and Diffusion Property</h3><div class="NLM_p">Moreover, it was reported that heterogeneous atom doping can make a great influence in the electronic structure and Li diffusion behavior on the graphene. Thus, it can be predicted that such a doping effect can also affect both the storage and rate behavior performances of G/N interfaces. In this section, we provide a systematic study on the doping effect at the interface of XG/N (where X represents the doping atom), which helps to further optimize the high electrochemical performance anode materials. Here, N, O, and S dopants are selected because they are the most widely studied in graphene experimentally. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50 ref51 ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 50 to 52">(50â52)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a, all of the doping atoms can increase the interface interaction energies between the graphene and Na<sub>2</sub>O. The <i>E</i><sub>I</sub> values for the NG/N and OG/N interfaces are still within the range of vdW force, which are consistent with the ELF analysis (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/suppl_file/nl9b00544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a> in the Supporting Information). However, the SG/N can induce the biggest value of 33.43 meV/Ã<sup>2</sup>. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b, the charge in the region near the S doping atom varies greatly, indicating that the SG/N interface is quite different from other interfaces (G/N, NG/N, OG/N). This can also be proved by the PDOS analysis (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/suppl_file/nl9b00544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a> in the Supporting Information), where it is clearly seen that the S-2p orbital electrons, O-2p electrons, and Na-2s electrons are intensively hybridized at the energy windows ranging from â3.2 eV to â1.3 eV. This indicates the existence of the covalent-type bonding, concentrating at the adjacent region of S atom and O atom. Bader charge analysis further shows that the S dopant loses electrons while the SG layer gains electrons, resulting in more electron accumulation at doped graphene and a stronger adhesion with Na<sub>2</sub>O surfaces. Therefore, S-doped graphene can induce the largest adhesive strength between graphene and Na<sub>2</sub>O, which can greatly improve the stability of the heterojunction-type negative materials during its charge and discharge cycle.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/asset/images/medium/nl-2019-00544t_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/asset/images/large/nl-2019-00544t_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Interfacial interaction energies, interface distance between the (N, O, and S)-doped G/N. (b) Charge density difference of SG/N. (c) Relationship between the sodiation voltage and the amount of Na adatoms (capacity) at different doped G/N interfaces. (d) Diffusion energy for Na diffusion along the interfacial migration path (shown in upper inset).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/asset/images/large/nl-2019-00544t_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The storage at doped G/N interface were also investigated. Our results show that the interface Na storage capabilities varies with the different dopants of the G/N interface, while the NG/N, OG/N and SG/N interface systems can adsorb 5, 6, and 4 Na atoms, respectively (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/suppl_file/nl9b00544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a> in the Supporting Information). For the OG/N system, the sodiation voltage go directly from 2 to 4, which were caused by the change of structures (see detailed discussion in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/suppl_file/nl9b00544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Section S3, Figure S14, and Table S2</a> in the Supporting Information). As discussed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec3_1" aria-label="Section 3.1">Section </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec3_1" aria-label="3.1">3.1</a>, the interfacial storage mechanism can be depicted as pseudocapacity-like interfacial behavior with charge separation, so the capability of the electron acceptance of graphene layer will play the decisive role on the interface Na storage (see detailed discussion in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/suppl_file/nl9b00544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Section S4 and Figure S15</a> in the Supporting Information). In order to study the doping effect on the rate performance of interfacial structure, the electronic structure and energy barrier of Na on the G/N interface were calculated. Although Na<sub>2</sub>O is a semiconductor with a direct band gap of 2.42 eV, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> there is always considerable state across the Fermi level when forming the composite structure of (N, O, S doped)-graphene/Na<sub>2</sub>O (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/suppl_file/nl9b00544_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16</a> in the Supporting Information). Combining with the good conductivity of pristine graphene, such interface structure can greatly improve the electronic conductivity of the materials. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d shows the doping effect on the diffuse performance of Na on the G/N interface. The results show that all doping systems via double-interstitialcy mechanism show smaller diffusion energy barrier than the value of pristine G/N interface (0.25 eV), corresponding to the 0.17 eV for NG/N, 0.13 eV for OG/N, and 0.11 eV for SG/N. The diffusion constant of S-doped systems is the largest among of them with 2.34 Ã 10<sup>â4</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>â1</sup> due to the smallest barrier, which is observably improved, compared with the pristine G/N interface and the diffusivity can be â¼3 orders faster. Combining with the interfacial stability, the storage capacity and the diffusion behavior of doping systems, the S-doping systems exhibits a good balance property among the three indicators, which show a good potential to further improve the electrical performance of G/MO materials.</div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">4.  Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, the interface interaction between the graphene and Na<sub>2</sub>O is mainly via van der Waals (vdW) interactions. The interface Na atom can be stored at the G/N interface through the pseudocapacity-like mechanism, which contributes to the interfacial capacity. By comparing the diffusion behaviors of Na atom on the Na<sub>2</sub>O bulk, the Na<sub>2</sub>O surface and the G/N interface, we found that the G/N interface can induce the ultrafast ion diffusivity, because of its unique âdouble interstitialcy mechanismâ. The experimental validation further demonstrated that the C/SnO<sub>2</sub>/C electrode material with high G/N interface ratio shows superior rated performance (338 mAh g<sup>â1</sup> at 0.1 A/g, 146 mAh g<sup>â1</sup> at 5 A/g, and 105 mAh g<sup>â1</sup> at 10 A/g) and the mixture storage mechanism. The high G/N interface structure is also beneficial to the reversible conversion of the active material during electrode cycling. In addition, the interfacial interaction, storage capacity, and diffusion behavior of XG/N (X = N, O, S) can be further regulated by heteroelement doping, while the O doping systems show the largest interfacial storage of 0.198 mAh/m<sup>2</sup> and the S-doping systems can greatly increase the interfacial interactions and decrease the diffusion barrier of the G/N interface by â¼3 order faster. Our work revealed the novel diffusion mechanism and unique pseudocapacity-like capacity behavior of G/MO by combing theoretical simulation and experiment validation, and meanwhile provided the promising strategy for designing G/MO anode materials with high storage and good rate performance, that is, increase three-dimensional (3D) graphene/metal oxide contact and select optimized doping atoms.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title"         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review is given.  Li-ion batteries have had a profound impact on our daily life, but inherent limitations make it difficult for Li-ion chemistries to meet the growing demands for portable electronics, elec. vehicles and grid-scale energy storage.  Therefore, chemistries beyond Li-ion are currently being investigated and need to be made viable for com. applications.  The use of metallic Li is one of the most favored choices for next-generation Li batteries, esp. Li-S and Li-air systems.  After falling into oblivion for several decades because of safety concerns, metallic Li is now ready for a revival, thanks to the development of investigative tools and nanotechnol.-based solns.  Here, we 1st summarize the current understanding on Li anodes, then highlight the recent key progress in materials design and advanced characterization techniques, and finally discuss the opportunities and possible directions for future development of Li anodes in applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr-8ZgW2ZC-SrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhIvB_bsLkiEA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXmtVyitr4%253D&amp;md5=07d29fc449ccc1f6c941b4c7692a8639</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1038/nmat4821"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gong, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fu, K. The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.  The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrZwDymuubZm7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhIvB_bsLkiEA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXhsVGktL%252FJ&amp;md5=5035bfda7631ad9d075e8836e61146ef</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200320"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vu, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Stein, A.</span></span> <i>Adv.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Porous electrode materials for lithium-ion batteries - how to prepare them and what makes them special</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Vu, Anh; Qian, Yuqiang; Stein, Andreas</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Numerous benefits of porous electrode materials for lithium ion batteries (LIBs) have been demonstrated, including examples of higher rate capabilities, better cycle lives, and sometimes greater gravimetric capacities at a given rate compared to nonporous bulk materials.  These properties promise advantages of porous electrode materials for LIBs in elec. and hybrid elec. vehicles, portable electronic devices, and stationary elec. energy storage.  This review highlights methods of synthesizing porous electrode materials by templating and template-free methods and discusses how the structural features of porous electrodes influence their electrochem. properties.  A section on electrochem. properties of porous electrodes provides examples that illustrate the influence of pore and wall architecture and interconnectivity, surface area, particle morphol., and nanocomposite formation on the utilization of the electrode materials, specific capacities, rate capabilities, and structural stability during lithiation and delithiation processes.  Recent applications of porous solids as components for three-dimensionally interpenetrating battery architectures are also described.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrR3Y5G3fAhCLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg2tcJ1mq7LpA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhsFags7rO&amp;md5=85d4c67c1d9ac4beee04147e773fef4e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1021/nn303145j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mukherjee, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Thomas, A. The development of new materials for Li-ion batteries is the focus of research in prominent groups in the field of materials science throughout the world.  Li-ion batteries can be considered to be the most impressive success story of modern electrochem. in the last two decades.  They power most of today's portable devices, and seem to overcome the psychol. barriers against the use of such high energy d. devices on a larger scale for more demanding applications, such as EV.  Since this field is advancing rapidly and attracting an increasing no. of researchers, it is important to provide current and timely updates of this constantly changing technol.  In this review, we describe the key aspects of Li-ion batteries: the basic science behind their operation, the most relevant components, anodes, cathodes, electrolyte solns., as well as important future directions for R&amp;D of advanced Li-ion batteries for demanding use, such as EV and load-leveling applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpS5wrQXqBLLbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg2tcJ1mq7LpA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXht1Cqs7jE&amp;md5=74c50c1f50dfffe2bb90d8e3aae4f157</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1021/cm403163c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Seo, D.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hollow bean-pod-like SiO2-supported-SnO2/C nanocomposites for durable lithium and sodium storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Linyu; Li, Site; Liu, Jun; Zhu, Kunjie; Liu, Sailin; Lei, Ming</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">In order to better resolve the large vol. expansion problem of SnO2-based anode materials, a hollow bean-pod-like SiO2-supported-SnO2/C nanostructure with a sufficient internal void space is designed.  Dual voids exist in this structure in combination with a SiO2 support in the core and C as an outside shell, providing triple guarantees to improve the performance of SnO2-based anode materials for Li and Na storage.  As a result, after 550 charge/discharge cycles, a discharge capacity of 649 mAh/g can still be obtained for Li storage, and a discharge capacity of 104 mAh/g can still be maintained after 1000 charge/discharge cycles for Na storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo6t1eL8hondrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg2tcJ1mq7LpA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XitVKjtLvK&amp;md5=ccdcfc5802b06928391648cf910b482d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1021/nl802484w"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Paek, S.                         cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Facile preparation of ordered porous graphene-metal oxide@C binder-free electrodes with high Li storage performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhu, Jixin; Yang, Dan; Rui, Xianhong; Sim, Daohao; Yu, Hong; Hoster, Harry E.; Ajayan, Pulickel M.; Yan, Qingyu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">20</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3390-3397</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SMALBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1613-6810</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hierarchically ordered porous graphene-metal oxide@C electrodes were prepd. using a facile and easily scalable method.  The structural features induced synergistic effects and leads to superior lithium storage properties.  These effects include a highly porous structure which allows for a large contact area between the electrolyte and electrode material; nanosized metal oxide walls greatly shorten the lithium diffusion paths; a thin carbon coating layer over the metal oxide wall wells alleviate the mech. strain; and the highly conductive graphene sheets ensure the fast charge carrier transport.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpfBgpqV9b19rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhCpcG40XZ-eA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXmtlaqt7w%253D&amp;md5=968c3bf4684647fab1aa77241566a084</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms2705"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, B.                         cas_cit28R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">28</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">SnS2/Sb2S3 Heterostructures Anchored on Reduced Graphene Oxide Nanosheets with Superior Rate Capability for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Shijian; Liu, Shuaishuai; Li, Xuemei; Li, Cong; Zang, Rui; Man, Zengming; Wu, Yuhan; Li, Pengxin; Wang, Guoxiu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry - A European Journal</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">24</span>
