        </div><div class="casAbstract">SnS2, as a promising high-capacity anode material for Na-ion batteries, exhibits high theor. capacity but poor practical electrochem. properties due to its low elec. cond.  Constructing heterostructures has been considered to be an effective approach to enhance charge transfer and ion-diffusion kinetics.  Here, composites of SnS2/Sb2S3 heterostructures with reduced graphene oxide nanosheets were synthesized by a facile 1-pot hydrothermal method.  When applied as anode material in Na-ion batteries, the composite showed a high reversible capacity of 642 mAh/g at a c.d. of 0.2 A/g and good cyclic stability without capacity loss in 100 cycles.  In particular, SnS2/Sb2S3 heterostructures exhibited outstanding rate performance with capacities of 593 and 567 mAh/g at high current densities of 2 and 4 A/g, resp., which could be ascribed to the dramatically improved Na+ diffusion kinetics and elec. cond.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqoNhp2mHbl4LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhCpcG40XZ-eA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXivFCrtL4%253D&amp;md5=ea5be3890494525fe0a28507015ced07</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit29" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201704670"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qin, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, C.</span></span> <i>Adv.                         cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Efficient iterative schemes for ab initio total-energy calculations using a plane-wave basis set</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kresse, G.; Furthmueller, J.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Review B: Condensed Matter</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1996</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">54</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Physical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors present an efficient scheme for calcg. the Kohn-Sham ground state of metallic systems using pseudopotentials and a plane-wave basis set.  In the first part the application of Pulay's DIIS method (direct inversion in the iterative subspace) to the iterative diagonalization of large matrixes will be discussed.  This approach is stable, reliable, and minimizes the no. of order Natoms3 operations.  In the second part, we will discuss an efficient mixing scheme also based on Pulay's scheme.  A special "metric" and a special "preconditioning" optimized for a plane-wave basis set will be introduced.  Scaling of the method will be discussed in detail for non-self-consistent and self-consistent calcns.  It will be shown that the no. of iterations required to obtain a specific precision is almost independent of the system size.  Altogether an order Natoms2 scaling is found for systems contg. up to 1000 electrons.  If we take into account that the no. of k points can be decreased linearly with the system size, the overall scaling can approach Natoms.  They have implemented these algorithms within a powerful package called VASP (Vienna ab initio simulation package).  The program and the techniques have been used successfully for a large no. of different systems (liq. and amorphous semiconductors, liq. simple and transition metals, metallic and semiconducting surfaces, phonons in simple metals, transition metals, and semiconductors) and turned out to be very reliable.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoK9e6g4dZiorVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljuMpHF-Nnb5w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaK28Xms1Whu7Y%253D&amp;md5=9c8f6f298fe5ffe37c2589d3f970a697</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit34" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevB.59.7413"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hammer, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hansen, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">NÃ¸rskov, J.</span></span> <i>Phys.                         cas_cit34R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">34</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Improved adsorption energetics within density-functional theory using revised Perdew-Burke-Ernzerhof functionals</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hammer, B.; Hansen, L. B.; Norskov, J. K.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Review B: Condensed Matter and Materials Physics</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A simple formulation of a generalized gradient approxn. for the exchange and correlation energy of electrons has been proposed by J. Perdew et al. (1996).  Subsequently, Y. Zhang and W. Wang (1998) have shown that a slight revision of the Perdew-Burke-Ernzerhof (PBE) functional systematically improves the atomization energies for a large database of small mols.  In the present work, we show that the Zhang and Yang functional (revPBE) also improves the chemisorption energetics of atoms and mols. on transition-metal surfaces.  Our test systems comprise at. and mol. adsorption of oxygen, CO, and NO on Ni(100), Ni(111), Rh(100), Pd(100), and Pd(111) surfaces.  As the revPBE functional may locally violate the Lieb-Oxford criterion, we further develop an alternative revision of the PBE functional, RPBE, which gives the same improvement of the chemisorption energies as the revPBE functional at the same time as it fulfills the Lieb-Oxford criterion locally.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGriGHUVH2AaC7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljuMpHF-Nnb5w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3cXjtlOgtA%253D%253D&amp;md5=5a79706aa2b3d959686cf4e425d21a6a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref35"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">35</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit35" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevB.90.155448"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hazrati, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">de Wijs, G.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Super-ionic conductor materials have great potential to enable novel technologies in energy storage and conversion.  However, it is not yet understood why only a few materials can deliver exceptionally higher ionic cond. than typical solids or how one can design fast ion conductors following simple principles.  Using ab initio modeling, here we show that fast diffusion in super-ionic conductors does not occur through isolated ion hopping as is typical in solids, but instead proceeds through concerted migrations of multiple ions with low energy barriers.  Furthermore, we elucidate that the low energy barriers of the concerted ionic diffusion are a result of unique mobile ion configurations and strong mobile ion interactions in super-ionic conductors.  Our results provide a general framework and universal strategy to design solid materials with fast ionic diffusion.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo9TuuEqQA1q7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgcLsZwzRS_zQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhtVeqtbjM&amp;md5=ca442e7d39979b975b99586daaabf1f9</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit42" data-doi="10.1002/anie.199718081"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Savin, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nesper, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wengert, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">FÃ¤ssler, T.                         cas_cit45R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">45</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sandwiched C@SnO2@C hollow nanostructures as an ultralong-lifespan high-rate anode material for lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Qin, Jian; Zhao, Naiqin; Shi, Chunsheng; Liu, Enzuo; He, Fang; Ma, Liying; Li, Qunying; Li, Jiajun; He, Chunnian</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">22</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">10946-10956</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">SnO2 is considered a promising anode candidate for both lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), but suffers from the low elec. cond. and severe vol. variation during repetitive cycling.  To circumvent these issues, we developed novel interconnected sandwiched carbon-coated hollow nanostructures, i.e., carbon-shell/SnO2-nanocrystal-layer/hollow-carbon-core (C@SnO2@C HNSs), in which ultrasmall SnO2 nanocrystals (2-5 nm) were tightly confined between the carbon shell and the hollow carbon core.  Such a unique structure can not only protect the active materials from direct exposure to the electrolyte as well as restrain the migration and pulverization of the SnO2, but also offer rich void space for buffering the vol. changes and complement the electron cond. of the active materials, thus achieving remarkably enhanced elec. cond. and structural integrity of the whole electrode.  As a consequence, this C@SnO2@C HNS electrode exhibited an extremely outstanding long-life high-rate cycling stability for both LIB and SIB anodes, such as only 8% capacity loss after 1000 cycles at 10 A g-1 for LIB anode and only 10% capacity loss after 3000 cycles at 4.6 A g-1 for SIB anodes.  As far as we know, this is the best high-rate cycle performance ever reported for SnO2-based SIB anodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqJcT2dXyJAc7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgcLsZwzRS_zQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXotVagtbs%253D&amp;md5=5c512107c207b360f0d59dfdf835af26</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref46"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">46</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit46" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201402943"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.                         cas_cit47R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">47</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodiation vs. lithiation phase transformations in a high rate - high stability SnO2 in carbon nanocomposite</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ding, Jia; Li, Zhi; Wang, Huanlei; Cui, Kai; Kohandehghan, Alireza; Tan, Xuehai; Karpuzov, Dimitre; Mitlin, David</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">13</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7100-7111</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A glucose mediated hydrothermal self-assembly method is employed to create a SnO2-carbon nanocomposite with promising electrochem. performance as both a sodium and a lithium ion battery anode (NIBs NABs SIBs, LIBs), being among the best in terms of cyclability and rate capability when tested against Na.  In parallel a systematic side-by-side comparison is provided of the sodiation vs. lithiation phase transformations in nano SnO2.  The high surface area (338 m2 g-1) electrode is named C-SnO2, and consists of a continuous Li and Na active carbon frame with internally imbedded sub-5 nm SnO2 crystallites of high mass loading (60 wt%).  The frame imparts excellent elec. cond. to the electrode, allows for rapid diffusion of Na and Li ions, and carries the sodiation/lithiation stresses while preventing cycling-induced agglomeration of the individual crystals.  The C-SnO2 employed as a NIB anode displays a reversible capacity of 531 mA h g-1 (at 0.08 A g-1) with 81% capacity retention after 200 cycles, while capacities of 240, 188, and 133 mA h g-1 are achieved at the much higher rates of 1.3, 2.6 and 5 A g-1.  As a LIB anode C-SnO2 maintains a capacity of 1367 mA h g-1 (at 0.5 A g-1) after 400 cycles, and 420 mA h g-1 at 10 A g-1.  Combined TEM, XRD and XPS prove that the much lower capacity of SnO2 as a NIB anode is due to the kinetic difficulty of the Na-Sn alloying reaction to reach the terminal Na15Sn4 intermetallic, whereas for Li-Sn the Li22Sn5 intermetallic is readily formed at 0.01 V.  Rather, with applied voltage a significant portion of the material effectively shuffles between SnO2 and Î²-Sn + NaO2.  The conversion reaction proceeds differently in the two systems: LiO2 is reduced directly to SnO2 and Li, whereas the NaO2 to SnO2 reaction proceeds through an intermediate SnO phase.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGozWPwIbsTBCLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgcLsZwzRS_zQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXjt1Sgs70%253D&amp;md5=83ec89f00a72a7b18e339aed621d1904</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit48" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms12122"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chao, D.                         cas_cit49R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">49</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ordered mesoporous Î±-MoO3 with iso-oriented nanocrystalline walls for thin-film pseudocapacitors</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Brezesinski, Torsten; Wang, John; Tolbert, Sarah H.; Dunn, Bruce</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2010</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">146-151</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NMAACR</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Capacitive energy storage is distinguished from other types of electrochem. energy storage by short charging times and the ability to deliver significantly more power than batteries.  A limitation to this technol. is its low energy d. and there is interest in exploring pseudo-capacitive materials where faradaic mechanisms offer increased energy storage.  The capacitive charge-storage properties of mesoporous films of iso-oriented Î±-MoO3 are superior to those of either mesoporous amorphous material or nonporous cryst. MoO3.  Whereas both cryst. and amorphous mesoporous materials show redox pseudo-capacitance, the iso-oriented layered cryst. domains enable Li ions to be inserted into the van der Waals gaps of the Î±-MoO3.  Probably this extra contribution arises from an intercalation pseudo-capacitance, which occurs on the same timescale as redox pseudo-capacitance.  The result is increased charge-storage capacity without compromising charge/discharge kinetics in mesoporous cryst. MoO3.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp77bOsasmjcrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgbiKjOjjxiPA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3cXhtVOnt78%253D&amp;md5=b6e743b60b27d6e0dfcabcf281d3d475</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref50"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">50</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit50" data-doi="10.1016/j.elecom.2017.02.015"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Klingele, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pham, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vuyyuru, K.                         cas_cit51R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">51</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Light non-metallic atom (B, N, O and F)-doped graphene: a first-principles study</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, M.; Cao, C.; Jiang, J. Z.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nanotechnology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2010</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">21</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">50</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">505202/1-505202/6</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Institute of Physics Publishing</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">First-principles calcns. are performed to study the geometry, electronic structure and magnetic properties of light nonmetallic atom-doped graphene (B, N, O and F).  The planar structure and the quasi-linear energy dispersion near the Dirac point remain through doping with B and N atoms, by which p-type doping and n-type doping graphene are resp. induced.  A bandgap of â¼0.5 eV is generated through O doping, and geometrically the O atom is also in the graphene plane.  No magnetic moment is detected in B-, N- and O-doped graphene.  For F doping, the F atom bonds with one of the carbon atoms close to the vacancy, with the other two carbon atoms undergoing a Jahn-Teller distortion.  A weak polarized magnetic moment of 0.71 Î¼B is detected through F doping.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoEWLKpjDJ1rrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgbiKjOjjxiPA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXjsVyisb4%253D&amp;md5=e35505912d23347ab3301ad392460442</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref52"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">52</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit52" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201400161"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Higgins, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hoque, M.                         cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electronic structure and ground-state properties of alkali-metal oxides - Li2O, Na2O, K2O and Rb2O: A first-principles study</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Eithiraj, R. D.; Jaiganesh, G.; Kalpana, G.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physica B: Condensed Matter (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2007</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">396</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1-2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">124-131</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">PHYBE3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0921-4526</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Self-consistent scalar-relativistic band structure calcns. were performed to investigate the electronic structure and ground-state properties of Li2O, Na2O, K2O and Rb2O in cubic antifluorite (anti-CaF2-type) structure using the TB-LMTO method.  The calcd. ground-state properties of these compds. such as equil. lattice parameter and bulk modulus are in agreement with the other theor. calcns. and exptl. results.  Li2O, K2O and Rb2O oxides are indirect-band-gap semiconductors, whereas Na2O is a direct-band-gap semiconductor.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq5_susWZzIrbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgbiKjOjjxiPA"> &gt;&gt; The green ball (labelled as âIâ) represents the interstitial Na atom, and the blue ball (labelled as âSâ) means the Na atom located in the lattice of Na<sub>2</sub>O.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/asset/images/large/nl-2019-00544t_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/asset/images/medium/nl-2019-00544t_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b00544/asset/images/large/nl-2019-00544t_0002.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr2" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review is given.  Li-ion batteries have had a profound impact on our daily life, but inherent limitations make it difficult for Li-ion chemistries to meet the growing demands for portable electronics, elec. vehicles and grid-scale energy storage.  Therefore, chemistries beyond Li-ion are currently being investigated and need to be made viable for com. applications.  The use of metallic Li is one of the most favored choices for next-generation Li batteries, esp. Li-S and Li-air systems.  After falling into oblivion for several decades because of safety concerns, metallic Li is now ready for a revival, thanks to the development of investigative tools and nanotechnol.-based solns.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Numerous benefits of porous electrode materials for lithium ion batteries (LIBs) have been demonstrated, including examples of higher rate capabilities, better cycle lives, and sometimes greater gravimetric capacities at a given rate compared to nonporous bulk materials.  These properties promise advantages of porous electrode materials for LIBs in elec. and hybrid elec. vehicles, portable electronic devices, and stationary elec. energy storage.  This review highlights methods of synthesizing porous electrode materials by templating and template-free methods and discusses how the structural features of porous electrodes influence their electrochem. properties.  A section on electrochem. properties of porous electrodes provides examples that illustrate the influence of pore and wall architecture and interconnectivity, surface area, particle morphol., and nanocomposite formation on the utilization of the electrode materials, specific capacities, rate capabilities, and structural stability during lithiation and delithiation processes. Li-ion batteries can be considered to be the most impressive success story of modern electrochem. in the last two decades.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In order to better resolve the large vol. expansion problem of SnO2-based anode materials, a hollow bean-pod-like SiO2-supported-SnO2/C nanostructure with a sufficient internal void space is designed.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Hierarchically ordered porous graphene-metal oxide@C electrodes were prepd. using a facile and easily scalable method.         </div><div class="casAbstract">SnS2, as a promising high-capacity anode material for Na-ion batteries, exhibits high theor. capacity but poor practical electrochem. properties due to its low elec. cond.  Constructing heterostructures has been considered to be an effective approach to enhance charge transfer and ion-diffusion kinetics.  Here, composites of SnS2/Sb2S3 heterostructures with reduced graphene oxide nanosheets were synthesized by a facile 1-pot hydrothermal method.  When applied as anode material in Na-ion batteries, the composite showed a high reversible capacity of 642 mAh/g at a c.d. of 0.2 A/g and good cyclic stability without capacity loss in 100 cycles.  In particular, SnS2/Sb2S3 heterostructures exhibited outstanding rate performance with capacities of 593 and 567 mAh/g at high current densities of 2 and 4 A/g, resp., which could be ascribed to the dramatically improved Na+ diffusion kinetics and elec. cond.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqoNhp2mHbl4LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh-YpPROoJEYA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXivFCrtL4%253D&amp;md5=ea5be3890494525fe0a28507015ced07</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref29" class="rightTabRefNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit29" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201704670"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qin, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, C.</span></span> <i>Adv.                         rightTab-cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Efficient iterative schemes for ab initio total-energy calculations using a plane-wave basis set</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kresse, G.; Furthmueller, J.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Review B: Condensed Matter</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1996</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Physical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors present an efficient scheme for calcg. the Kohn-Sham ground state of metallic systems using pseudopotentials and a plane-wave basis set.  In the first part the application of Pulay's DIIS method (direct inversion in the iterative subspace) to the iterative diagonalization of large matrixes will be discussed.  This approach is stable, reliable, and minimizes the no. of order Natoms3 operations.  In the second part, we will discuss an efficient mixing scheme also based on Pulay's scheme.  A special "metric" and a special "preconditioning" optimized for a plane-wave basis set will be introduced.  Scaling of the method will be discussed in detail for non-self-consistent and self-consistent calcns.  It will be shown that the no. of iterations required to obtain a specific precision is almost independent of the system size.  Altogether an order Natoms2 scaling is found for systems contg. up to 1000 electrons.  If we take into account that the no. of k points can be decreased linearly with the system size, the overall scaling can approach Natoms.  They have implemented these algorithms within a powerful package called VASP (Vienna ab initio simulation package).  The program and the techniques have been used successfully for a large no. of different systems (liq. and amorphous semiconductors, liq. simple and transition metals, metallic and semiconducting surfaces, phonons in simple metals, transition metals, and semiconductors) and turned out to be very reliable.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoK9e6g4dZiorVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh-YpPROoJEYA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaK28Xms1Whu7Y%253D&amp;md5=9c8f6f298fe5ffe37c2589d3f970a697</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref34" class="rightTabRefNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit34" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevB.59.7413"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hammer, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hansen, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">NÃ¸rskov, J.</span></span> <i>Phys.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A simple formulation of a generalized gradient approxn. for the exchange and correlation energy of electrons has been proposed by J. Perdew et al. (1996).  Subsequently, Y. Zhang and W. Wang (1998) have shown that a slight revision of the Perdew-Burke-Ernzerhof (PBE) functional systematically improves the atomization energies for a large database of small mols.  In the present work, we show that the Zhang and Yang functional (revPBE) also improves the chemisorption energetics of atoms and mols. on transition-metal surfaces.  Our test systems comprise at. and mol. adsorption of oxygen, CO, and NO on Ni(100), Ni(111), Rh(100), Pd(100), and Pd(111) surfaces.  As the revPBE functional may locally violate the Lieb-Oxford criterion, we further develop an alternative revision of the PBE functional, RPBE, which gives the same improvement of the chemisorption energies as the revPBE functional at the same time as it fulfills the Lieb-Oxford criterion locally.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGriGHUVH2AaC7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh-YpPROoJEYA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3cXjtlOgtA%253D%253D&amp;md5=5a79706aa2b3d959686cf4e425d21a6a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref35"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref35" class="rightTabRefNumLink">35</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit35" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevB.90.155448"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hazrati, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">de Wijs, G.     </div><div class="casAbstract">The perturbation theory in a gage field theory of quarks [12585-73-8] and gluons [11104-72-6] can be used to calc. phys. mass-shell processes, provided that both the data and the theory are smeared over a suitable energy range.  This procedure is explored in detail for the process of e--e+ annihilation into hadrons [12585-58-9].  A smearing range of 3 GeV2 in the squared center-of-mass energy should be adequate to allow the use of lowest-order perturbation theory.  The smeared data are compared with theory for a variety of models.  It appears to be difficult to fit the present data with theory, unless there is a new charged lepton or quark with mass in the region 2-3 GeV.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqXLfhjonE5ubVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh-YpPROoJEYA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaE28XksFejsb8%253D&amp;md5=b7f4bb7b53f605d027c6af236466c725</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref37" class="rightTabRefNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit37" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevLett.101.036808"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lherbier, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Blase, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Niquet, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Super-ionic conductor materials have great potential to enable novel technologies in energy storage and conversion.  However, it is not yet understood why only a few materials can deliver exceptionally higher ionic cond. than typical solids or how one can design fast ion conductors following simple principles.  Using ab initio modeling, here we show that fast diffusion in super-ionic conductors does not occur through isolated ion hopping as is typical in solids, but instead proceeds through concerted migrations of multiple ions with low energy barriers.  Furthermore, we elucidate that the low energy barriers of the concerted ionic diffusion are a result of unique mobile ion configurations and strong mobile ion interactions in super-ionic conductors.  Our results provide a general framework and universal strategy to design solid materials with fast ionic diffusion.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo9TuuEqQA1q7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgCjT0hHGN3DA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhtVeqtbjM&amp;md5=ca442e7d39979b975b99586daaabf1f9</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref42" class="rightTabRefNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit42" data-doi="10.1002/anie.199718081"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Savin, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nesper, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wengert, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">FÃ¤ssler, T.         </div><div class="casAbstract">SnO2 is considered a promising anode candidate for both lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), but suffers from the low elec. cond. and severe vol. variation during repetitive cycling.                         rightTab-cas_cit47R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">47</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodiation vs. lithiation phase transformations in a high rate - high stability SnO2 in carbon nanocomposite</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ding, Jia; Li, Zhi; Wang, Huanlei; Cui, Kai; Kohandehghan, Alireza; Tan, Xuehai; Karpuzov, Dimitre; Mitlin, David</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A glucose mediated hydrothermal self-assembly method is employed to create a SnO2-carbon nanocomposite with promising electrochem. performance as both a sodium and a lithium ion battery anode (NIBs NABs SIBs, LIBs), being among the best in terms of cyclability and rate capability when tested against Na.  In parallel a systematic side-by-side comparison is provided of the sodiation vs. lithiation phase transformations in nano SnO2.  The high surface area (338 m2 g-1) electrode is named C-SnO2, and consists of a continuous Li and Na active carbon frame with internally imbedded sub-5 nm SnO2 crystallites of high mass loading (60 wt%).  The frame imparts excellent elec. cond. to the electrode, allows for rapid diffusion of Na and Li ions, and carries the sodiation/lithiation stresses while preventing cycling-induced agglomeration of the individual crystals.  The C-SnO2 employed as a NIB anode displays a reversible capacity of 531 mA h g-1 (at 0.08 A g-1) with 81% capacity retention after 200 cycles, while capacities of 240, 188, and 133 mA h g-1 are achieved at the much higher rates of 1.3, 2.6 and 5 A g-1.  As a LIB anode C-SnO2 maintains a capacity of 1367 mA h g-1 (at 0.5 A g-1) after 400 cycles, and 420 mA h g-1 at 10 A g-1.  Combined TEM, XRD and XPS prove that the much lower capacity of SnO2 as a NIB anode is due to the kinetic difficulty of the Na-Sn alloying reaction to reach the terminal Na15Sn4 intermetallic, whereas for Li-Sn the Li22Sn5 intermetallic is readily formed at 0.01 V.  Rather, with applied voltage a significant portion of the material effectively shuffles between SnO2 and Î²-Sn + NaO2.  The conversion reaction proceeds differently in the two systems: LiO2 is reduced directly to SnO2 and Li, whereas the NaO2 to SnO2 reaction proceeds through an intermediate SnO phase.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGozWPwIbsTBCLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgCjT0hHGN3DA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXjt1Sgs70%253D&amp;md5=83ec89f00a72a7b18e339aed621d1904</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref48" class="rightTabRefNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit48" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms12122"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chao, D. H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, X. H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.                 10.1038/ncomms12122</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref49" class="rightTabRefNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit49" data-doi="10.1038/nmat2612"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Brezesinski, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tolbert, S. H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dunn, B.</span></span> <i>Nat. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2010</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>9</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">146</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1038/nmat2612</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit49R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">49</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ordered mesoporous Î±-MoO3 with iso-oriented nanocrystalline walls for thin-film pseudocapacitors</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Brezesinski, Torsten; Wang, John; Tolbert, Sarah H.; Dunn, Bruce</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2010</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">146-151</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NMAACR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1476-1122</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Capacitive energy storage is distinguished from other types of electrochem. energy storage by short charging times and the ability to deliver significantly more power than batteries.  A limitation to this technol. is its low energy d. and there is interest in exploring pseudo-capacitive materials where faradaic mechanisms offer increased energy storage.  The capacitive charge-storage properties of mesoporous films of iso-oriented Î±-MoO3 are superior to those of either mesoporous amorphous material or nonporous cryst. MoO3.  Whereas both cryst. and amorphous mesoporous materials show redox pseudo-capacitance, the iso-oriented layered cryst. domains enable Li ions to be inserted into the van der Waals gaps of the Î±-MoO3.  Probably this extra contribution arises from an intercalation pseudo-capacitance, which occurs on the same timescale as redox pseudo-capacitance.  The result is increased charge-storage capacity without compromising charge/discharge kinetics in mesoporous cryst. MoO3.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp77bOsasmjcrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgCjT0hHGN3DA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3cXhtVOnt78%253D&amp;md5=b6e743b60b27d6e0dfcabcf281d3d475</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref50"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref50" class="rightTabRefNumLink">50</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit50" data-doi="10.1016/j.elecom.2017.02.015"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Klingele, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pham, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vuyyuru, K. R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Britton, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Holdcroft, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fischer, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Thiele, S.</span></span> <i>Electrochem.                 10.1016/j.elecom.2017.02.015</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="

Z.</span></span> <i>Nanotechnology</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2010</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>21</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">505202</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1088/0957-4484/21/50/505202</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit51R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">51</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Light non-metallic atom (B, N, O and F)-doped graphene: a first-principles study</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, M.; Cao, C.; Jiang, J. Z.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nanotechnology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2010</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">21</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">50</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">505202/1-505202/6</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NNOTER</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1361-6528</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Institute of Physics Publishing</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">First-principles calcns. are performed to study the geometry, electronic structure and magnetic properties of light nonmetallic atom-doped graphene (B, N, O and F).  The planar structure and the quasi-linear energy dispersion near the Dirac point remain through doping with B and N atoms, by which p-type doping and n-type doping graphene are resp. induced.  A bandgap of â¼0.5 eV is generated through O doping, and geometrically the O atom is also in the graphene plane.  No magnetic moment is detected in B-, N- and O-doped graphene.  For F doping, the F atom bonds with one of the carbon atoms close to the vacancy, with the other two carbon atoms undergoing a Jahn-Teller distortion.  A weak polarized magnetic moment of 0.71 Î¼B is detected through F doping.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoEWLKpjDJ1rrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgCjT0hHGN3DA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXjsVyisb4%253D&amp;md5=e35505912d23347ab3301ad392460442</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref52"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref52" class="rightTabRefNumLink">52</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit52" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201400161"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Higgins, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hoque, M. D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jaiganesh, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kalpana, G.</span></span> <i>Phys. B</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2007</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>396</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">124</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.physb.2007.03.024</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electronic structure and ground-state properties of alkali-metal oxides - Li2O, Na2O, K2O and Rb2O: A first-principles study</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Eithiraj, R. D.; Jaiganesh, G.; Kalpana, G.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physica B: Condensed Matter (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2007</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">396</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1-2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">124-131</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Self-consistent scalar-relativistic band structure calcns. were performed to investigate the electronic structure and ground-state properties of Li2O, Na2O, K2O and Rb2O in cubic antifluorite (anti-CaF2-type) structure using the TB-LMTO method.  The calcd. ground-state properties of these compds. such as equil. lattice parameter and bulk modulus are in agreement with the other theor. calcns. and exptl. results.  Li2O, K2O and Rb2O oxides are indirect-band-gap semiconductors, whereas Na2O is a direct-band-gap semiconductor.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq5_susWZzIrbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh2-qTsIvl_UQ"> &gt;&gt; (function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	});

<script nonce="90f6e3011a13775c-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e3011a13775c-SJC">
"use strict"
//Script checks gdpr cookie and submits form
	/* 
	Create an in-page pop-up message, only for the InfoCentral homepage, that will gather email registrants for the library marketing list.
	 
	Needs the end point
	*/

var infoCentralCapture = (function() {

    //Detect mobile device
    window.mobilecheck = function() {

    var _isMobile = window.mobilecheck();
    if (_isMobile) {
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').addClass('mobile');
    }

    var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage = "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

    function init() {
        setTimeout(function() {
            var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
            if (cookieExist) {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
                return false;
            } else {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
                gdprMessage();
            }
            //Events
            $('#closebtn').on("click", hideForm);

            //Handle submit
            $('#gdpr-message-btn').on("click", function(event) {
                handleClick();
            });

            // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
            // $(document).click(function(event) {
            // 	event.stopPropagation();
            //   console.log(event.target);
            //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
            //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
            //     $(this).off();
            //   }
            // });
            $('#gdpr-con-btn').click(function() {
                setCookie("gdpr", "true");
                hideForm();
            });
        }, time);
    }

    //Show the message
    function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $('#gdpr-message-modal').show();
    }

    function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
            //Submit the form
            //Hide form
            if ($('.gdpr_err')) {
                $('.gdpr_err').hide();
            }

            $("#gdpr-message-content")
                //If successful then...
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
            //Show error message
            gdprErrorMessage();
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
    }

    function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
            return false;
        } else {
            errorStatus = true;
            $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
    }

    function validateEmail(email) {
        var re = /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
    }

    function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
    }

    function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + '=([^;]+)'));
        if (match) return match[1];
    }

    function hideForm() { //And set cookie
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag('event', 'GDPR Modal', { 'event_category': 'Pubs GDPR', 'event_label':'GDPR Dismissal'});
    }

    return {
        init: init
    }
})();

$(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
</script>


                    
                        



        
        <script nonce="90f6e3011a13775c-SJC">
var currentJournal = "Nano Letters";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>

$(document).ready(function(){
    var cookie = "";
    var uri = "/doi/full/10.1021/html_original_uri?id=123";
    console.log("ACSPubs2 start");
    $.getJSON('https://extreme-ip-lookup.com/json/', function(data, status) {
            if ( status == 'success' ) {
                $.each(data, function(i, field){
                    cookie=cookie.concat(i+' = '+field+" - ");
                });
                
            } else {
                    cookie=status;
            }

            cookie1 = cookie;
            cookie = cookie1 + "; path=/; domain=.acs.org";
            //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
            $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
            //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
            console.log("ACSPubs2 success");
    })
    .fail(function(xx, status, err){
        cookie="error: 127.0.0.1";
        //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
        $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
        console.log("ACSPubs2 failed");
        //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
    });
});
